Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sluggish reaction kinetics induced by the poor solid-state ion diffusion and low electrical conductivity of electrode materials are currently in conflict with increasing fast-charge needs for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) based on conversion mechanism. Herein, mesoporous, conductive, thin-wall three-dimensional (3D) skeletons of molybdenum nitride (meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N) were established and employed as anodes to facilitate the rate performance of SIBs. Mesoporous channels (∼9.3 nm) with very thin walls (&lt;8 nm) and conductive networks in meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N enable the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> infiltrability/diffusion and fast electron migration, respectively. The facilitated ion diffusion/transfer ability is corroborated by cyclic voltammetry tests and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique with a higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and a larger Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion-dominated capacity. Consequently, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N exhibits a superior rate capability and a steady specific capacity of 158 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles for SIBs, surpassing the nonporous Mo<sub>2</sub>N and even the previously reported Mo<sub>2</sub>N. Furthermore, the proof of concept can be also extended to enhanced Li storage. Such a mesostructured design with 3D mesoporous, conductive thin walls of electrodes is a promising strategy for achieving fast-charging and high-performance Na/Li storage.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D41188%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DGuangshen%2BJiang%252C%2BYuqian%2BQiu%252C%2BQiongqiong%2BLu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D44%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b07060%26title%3DMesoporous%2BThin-Wall%2BMolybdenum%2BNitride%2Bfor%2BFast%2Band%2BStable%2BNa%252FLi%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D41195%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292111&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nitrides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nitrides" class="article__tags__link">Nitrides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Molybdenum+Nitride&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_molybdenum%20nitride">molybdenum nitride</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Thin+Wall&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_thin%20wall">thin wall</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fast+Charging&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_fast%20charging">fast charging</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lithium-ion%20batteries">lithium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Rapid charging demands are put on the agenda with exploding utilization of electrochemical energy devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Therefore, exploiting advanced energy storage devices with rapid charge/discharge capability and long lifespan is pressing <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> and of paramount significance for promoting next electric vehicles and portable electronics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have garnered enormous attention in view of the widespread distribution, low cost, and natural abundance of Na (2.3 wt % in Earth’s crust vs 0.0017 wt % of Li). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> As a result, they are deemed as one class of emulative batteries for the future large-scale energy storage systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> Despite this, one of the major obstructions for the exploration of high-performance SIBs is the deficiency of suitable anode electrode materials. This is probably because Na<sup>+</sup> has a larger radius (1.02 Å) compared with that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> implying more sluggish diffusion kinetics during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Hence, boosting Na<sup>+</sup> (de)sodiation kinetics and enhancing capacity is a key for their pending prevalent applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">As is known, the diffusion time (τ) could be depicted in equation τ = <i>L</i><sup>2</sup>/<i>D</i> (<i>L</i> and <i>D</i> represent the diffusion distance and the diffusion coefficient, respectively). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> From that, downsizing electrode materials could cut down the ion-diffusion distance and correspondingly enhance the conversion kinetics. Consequently, tremendous efforts are devoted to exploring variously nanostructured transition-metal compounds, such as oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> sulfides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and selenides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> to tackle the challenging issues of anode materials in SIBs. However, they have poor conductivity in nature. To heighten their conductivity, many types of carbon matrix (e.g., carbon nanotubes or graphene) are utilized to host these compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> However, this would give rise to the loss of specific capacity based on the whole electrode because carbon materials generally contribute less capacity based on (de)intercalation chemistry. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Intriguingly, transition-metal nitrides have aroused great attention for the merits of their large theoretical specific capacity (e.g., 390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Mo<sub>2</sub>N) and splendid conductivity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> such as molybdenum nitride (Mo<sub>2</sub>N). Nevertheless, unlike metal oxides and sulfides with rich nanostructure morphology, most of the as-synthesized Mo<sub>2</sub>N are limited in the forms of films, nanoparticles, nanowires, or nanobelts till now. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 to 26">(23−26)</a> Although advances have been made to enhance the electrochemical properties using these Mo<sub>2</sub>N with dimensions ranging from 0 to 2, they still displayed unsatisfactory specific capacity and inferior electrochemical properties because of low specific surface area and long diffusion distance for Na<sup>+</sup> when applied as an anode in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 to 26">(23−26)</a> Consequently, further enhancement could still be expected when the introduction of three-dimensional (3D) porous nanostructure into metallic Mo<sub>2</sub>N is taken into account.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, a unique three-dimensional (3D) mesoporous thin-wall Mo<sub>2</sub>N (meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N) was successfully fabricated via template-assisted approach. In our constructed porous Mo<sub>2</sub>N skeletons, the thin walls with a thickness of ∼7.5 nm for shortening transport route of Na<sup>+</sup> and the inherent outstanding conductivity for promoting rapid electron transfer jointly accelerate the (de)sodiation reaction kinetics. Additionally, the space encircled with nanoframeworks effectively tolerates volume variations induced by repeated (de)sodiation, as compared with nonporous Mo<sub>2</sub>N (bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N). Impressively, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N displays a steady specific capacity of 158 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> even after cycling 1000 times for SIBs, outperforming the nonporous ones. Furthermore, the advantages of 3D mesoporous design in Mo<sub>2</sub>N can be also extended to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with specific capacity of 350 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after cycling 2000 times. The prominent cycle stability and rate capability indicates that these thin-wall mesostructured nitrides electrodespose great opportunity for energy storage devices with fast-charging capability.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a depicts the detailed fabrication process of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. Ammonium molybdate tetrahydrate ((NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub>Mo<sub>7</sub>O<sub>24</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, AMT) as a molybdenum source is initially impregnated into the mesoporous silica template (scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a,d</a>), then decomposes into MoO<sub>3</sub> after calcination. Further  nitridation under NH<sub>3</sub> atmosphere with subsequent template removal via HF etching yields meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>). The ultimate phase was monitored by X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurement. The Bragg characteristic peaks located at the (111), (200), (220), (311), and (222) planes are well-matched to the Mo<sub>2</sub>N phase with <i>Pm</i>3<i>m</i> (221) space group (JCPDS: 25-1366), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> and no peak ascribed to metallic Mo or other molybdenum nitrides exists in the XRD patterns, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. These results imply that all the molybdenum species were evolved to Mo<sub>2</sub>N by the nitridation procedure. The pore structures of the as-obtained meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N were investigated via N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption test. Evidently, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N reveals the typical IV type isotherm bearing a marked H<sub>1</sub> hysteresis loop within the <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> range of 0.65–0.9 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), demonstrating its mesoporous characteristics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Also, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N possesses a high Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 87 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> with the pore volume of 0.25 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and pore size centered at 9.3 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1 and Figure S1e</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic of the fabrication of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (b) XRD patterns of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (d) TEM image and (e) high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (inset: selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern) of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (f) Crystalline structure of Mo<sub>2</sub>N.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the mesoporous property, TEM test was performed. Evident 3D networklike skeletons are shown for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d), with wall thickness of merely 7.4–7.6 nm. Therefore, such interconnected thin walls help to reduce the ion-migration distance effectively in the solid phase and thereby promote the insertion/extraction kinetics, allowing most of Mo<sub>2</sub>N to be efficiently utilized during cycling. The lattice fringe of 2.36 and 2.04 Å, which are indexed to the (111) and (200) planes within the crystalline Mo<sub>2</sub>N, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref29" aria-label="reference 25 and 29">(25,29)</a> correspondingly, are clearly displayed in the HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e), further validating the formation of Mo<sub>2</sub>N. The representative SAED patterns of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e exhibit annular patterns, indicating the polycrystalline nature of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f, Mo<sub>2</sub>N has a lamellar crystal structure. As electrode materials, the electron conductivity is of vital importance for smooth electrochemical reaction. meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N has a higher electrical conductivity of 1 × 10<sup>5</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> As expected, mesoporous, thin-wall, and conductive meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N was successfully constructed.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na storage behaviors of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N were investigated in half-cell configuration with Na foils as the counter electrodes. To highlight the merits of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N as electrodes, bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N as a control was also prepared by the direct decomposition and subsequent nitridation of AMT (XRD pattern shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N has a little N<sub>2</sub> adsorption amount with the BET surface area of only 9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> in contrast with meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), corroborating its nonporous nature. The TEM image further reveals the presence of a solid block in bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1f</a>). In the initial cathodic scan of cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, an observable peak emerges at ∼1.27 V and a broad one at an interval ranging from 0.33 to 0.7 V, representing the probable phase transformation from Mo<sub>2</sub>N to the intermedium phase of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mo<sub>2</sub>N and the final reduction to metal Mo<sup>0</sup> with the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>N under continuous Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> Also, another peak is present at ∼0.12 V, which is assigned to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film and Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into the conductive additive carbon Super P, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 21, 24 and 25">(21,24,25)</a> and disappears in the successive scanning. The oxidative peaks of Mo species located at ∼0.47 and ∼0.71 V in the desodiation process probably signify the conversion of metallic Mo<sup>0</sup> to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mo<sub>2</sub>N and then to Mo<sub>2</sub>N in the anodic scan. After the first two cycles, the peaks emerging at 1.27 and 0.53 V in the first cycle shift to lower potentials at ∼0.68 and ∼0.28 V in the subsequent cycling (from 3rd to 5th), probably caused by initial complicated processes like the formation of slight oxidation layer, the activation, and the irreversible reaction. The repeated CV curves almost overlap, suggesting the prominent electrochemical reversibility and structural stability of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. Although bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N wholly displays the similar CV curves to those of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, the evident peaks between 1.5 and 2.3 V appear only in the oxidative process of the first two cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>), probably arising from the breakage of SEI layer. This situation only occurs in bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, not in mesoporous one. Owing to the deficiency of space to buffer repeated volume fluctuation, SEI film of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N suffers from a fracture during the initial (de)sodiation process, meaning that it has less stability than that of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. To gain a deeper insight into the reaction process, the phases of Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes at various charge/discharge states were investigated in detail by ex situ XRD tests (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Before discharging (I), only the characteristic peaks of Mo<sub>2</sub>N located at 37.4, 43.5, and 63.1° (JCPDS: 25-1366) are observed. Upon discharging to 1.27 V (II), Na<sub>3</sub>N and Mo<sup>0</sup> (JCPDS: 42-1120) phases emerge, implying the occurrence of electrochemical conversion of partial Mo<sub>2</sub>N. At the final discharge state of 0.01 V (III), the peak located at 63.1° almost vanishes and only peaks for Na<sub>3</sub>N and Mo<sup>0</sup> are present, indicating the complete conversion of Mo<sub>2</sub>N. In the charging process, the peak of Mo<sub>2</sub>N phase reappears at 0.71 V (IV) and the peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>N and Mo<sup>0</sup> are further weakened. At the fully charged state of 3.0 V (V), Mo species completely reconvert to Mo<sub>2</sub>N, verifying the reversible reaction process. However, the peak at 43.5° for Mo<sub>2</sub>N has a slight shift after the discharge/charge process, probably arising from lattice changes induced by the conversion reaction in Mo<sub>2</sub>N. In all tested stages, the peaks located at 43.7 and 50.4° are indexed to Cu<sup>0</sup> (current collector). Therefore, the Na storage mechanism in Mo<sub>2</sub>N can be depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. In SIBs, (a) CV curves of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Third galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Ex situ XRD patterns of Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes at different charge/discharge states as indicated in the corresponding voltage profile. (d) Proposed Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in Mo<sub>2</sub>N.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The compare of the third galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N displays not only a higher specific capacity but also a smaller polarization of 1.41 V. The initial galvanostatic discharge curve of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as revealed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, displays two quasi-plateaus located at ∼1.25 and ∼0.55 V, prolongating to 0.01 V, consistent with the CV tests. Impressively, the profiles show the first sodiation and desodiation capacities of 416/223 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the corresponding Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 54%. Like many anode materials, the loss of the nonreversible capacity should be assigned to the electrolyte decomposition and the subsequent formation of the SEI layer over the electrode surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 19 and 33 to 35">(19,33−35)</a> From 100th to 500th cycle, the charge/discharge curves of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N overlap very well, manifesting its stable structure and electrochemical reversibility. As for bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, it delivers a capacity of only 86 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an inferior CE of 45.1%, as revealed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>. For meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, the reversible specific capacity stabilizes at 193 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after cycling 500 times, indicative of a high  capacity retention of 99.6% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). A capacity of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N rises to 127 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the initial 30 cycles, in stark contrast, but ultimately reduces to 44 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with dramatically fluctuated CE under the same conditions, which likely suffers from severe volume variations inducing the instability of electrodes. meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N displays a superior cycling performance in contrast with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, highlighting the significance of the rational and elaborate design of electrode materials. With meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode thickness increasing from ∼10, 16, to 18 μm, the areal capacity increases from 0.208 to 0.232 and then decreases to 0.181 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>). Nevertheless, at the same current density, the specific capacity decreases from 177, 143, to 119 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the mass loading increasing from 1.0, 1.4, to 1.8 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>). The worse electrolyte infiltrability and the increasing resistance of charge transportation due to the thickening electrode probably lead to the discrepancy in specific capacity.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (b) Galvanostatic cycling performances at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate performance of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (d) Comparison of electrochemical performance for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and previously reported metal nitrides based electrodes in SIBs. (e) Long-cycle performance of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N at 1 and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For practical applications, the fast charge/discharge performances are important. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref36" aria-label="reference 4 and 36">(4,36)</a> Thus, the rate capability of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N was investigated, which shows a considerable enhancement in comparison with that of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N displays higher capacities of 215, 186, 160, 146, 136, and 114 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at every gradient current density from 0.1 to 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> compared with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. More importantly, the capacity quickly recovers its pristine level of 216 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating a marvelous reversibility, once the current density is reverted to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c,d</a> reveal the typical discharge/charge curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes at varying current densities. As for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, the discrepancies between charge/discharge curves become slightly larger, but the voltage plateaus remain readily distinguishable even at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, further indicating the prominent rate capability. The excellent performance of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N over that of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N would mainly be attributed to the interconnected conductive mesostructure with thin walls, which enhances the electrolyte ion diffusion/transfer and increases the exposed active sites between Mo<sub>2</sub>N and electrolyte, resulting in an enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> motion and accessibility. Additionally, at  higher current densities of 1 and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacities of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N show almost no degradation within 1000 cycles with a rather stable value of 158 and 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). Also, the CE remains over 99% during the whole cycle. Obviously, for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, its electrochemical performance is superior to that of the reported porous Mo<sub>2</sub>N nanobelts. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a>  The cycling capability is also superior to those of previously the reported metal nitrides (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). These results also indicate that, at high current densities, the meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode can still deliver satisfying capacity and prolonged lifespan, which is a requisite for achieving high-performance SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">The variations of the thickness of sodiated (after 500 cycles) and pristine electrodes were investigated to verify the role of mesostructure for buffering repeated volume changes during discharging/charging process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a–d displays an increase of about 10% (from 10 to 11 μm) in the thickness of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode, lower than that of the bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode (25%, from 12 to 15 μm), indicating the good structural steadiness and the outstanding buffering capability in volume fluctuation for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. When porous skeletons of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode are almost in sodiation state (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), Na<sup>+</sup> is inserted not only on the surface but also in the interior of Mo<sub>2</sub>N through mesochannels, leading to a limited volume change during the conversion reaction. Three-dimensional mesoporous thin-wall frameworks, as schematically revealed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e, enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and alleviate volume expansion, which is responsible for enhancing the cycling capacity, rate capability, and structural stability. The morphologies of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes before and after 500 cycles were further investigated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a,b</a>). The plain and smooth surface representing the SEI film could be observed in meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, whereas many rough and cracked bulges appear in the SEI layer for bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c,d</a>). Taken together, the design of the mesoporous structure of Mo<sub>2</sub>N reduces ion diffusion/transfer resistance, buffers volume change, and stabilizes the SEI layer.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cross-sectional SEM images of  meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes (pristine electrodes): (a) meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and (c) bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N; fully sodiation state after 500 cycles: (b) meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and (d) bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (e) Schematic of the volume fluctuation during the electrochemical charge/discharge process involved in meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To deeply analyze the thermodynamics and kinetics of (de)sodiation in the electrode material, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was utilized. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> At each relaxation period of the GITT test, the potential changes of discharge/charge represent the overpotentials at various Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction stages, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> as revealed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. During whatever discharge/charge process, in general, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N displays smaller overpotentials in comparison with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. Especially, in the higher voltage interval, the disparity is more marked than that of the lower interval, meaning more favorable thermodynamics in electrochemical conversion for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Meanwhile, the reaction internal resistances (derived from the calculation of dividing overpotential by pulse current) are employed to evaluate the (de)sodiation kinetics of these two electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref41" aria-label="reference 37 and 41">(37,41)</a> Except for the approximate internal resistances during the insertion-end and the extraction-start-up stages, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N shows lower reaction resistances within the other sodiation and desodiation stages, as compared with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). All in all, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N can facilitate a reversible electrochemical conversion from thermodynamic and kinetics aspects.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. In SIBs, (a) GITT profiles of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N with relaxation for 4 h after 1 h discharge/charge at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Internal resistances between charge and discharge steps after the 4 h relaxation for these two electrodes. (c) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Logarithm peak current versus logarithm scan rate plots (inset: a summary of the calculated <i>b</i> values) for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (e) Capacitive contribution at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Diffusion-dominated contribution at different scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In general, the capacity stems from the contributions of both diffusion-controlled ((de)sodiation process) and capacitive parts. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Regarded as a rate-determining step for the whole electrochemical reaction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> solid-state ion diffusion is a bottleneck for high-performance and fast-charging batteries. To corroborate the higher capability of the solid-state diffusion within meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, we quantitatively analyzed the effects of diffusion-dominated and capacitive fractions by CV curves at varying sweep speed (from 0.1 to 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) of Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes. Obviously, all the CV profiles have resembling shapes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5a</a>). The peak current has a logarithm relationship with the scan rate plots, reckoning from the CV data in accordance with the following equations <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup> or log <i>i</i> = log <i>a</i> + <i>b</i> log <i>v</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Here, <i>i</i> is the peak current, <i>v</i> the scan rate, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> constants. Generally ranging from 0.5 (diffusion-dominated process) to 1 (capacitive process), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> the <i>b</i> value can evaluate the slope by plotting log(<i>i</i>) against log(<i>v</i>). As for Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes, the <i>b</i> values (e.g., meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N peak A: 0.865, peak C: 0.884) are between 0.5 and 1.0, implying the coexistence of diffusion-controlled and capacitive processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5b</a>). Moreover, the relationship between the current response <i>i</i> and the sweeping rates is expressed as in the equation <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> at a given potential <i>V</i>. Here, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are the constants calculated at varying scan rates. <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> are the corresponding capacitive and diffusion-controlled contributions. The typical CV profile of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for the current from capacitive (orange area) and diffusion-dominated processes (green area) is clearly shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. A diffusion-dominatedcontribution is 57.3%, higher than that of 47.6% of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. Likewise, the fractions of diffusion-dominated contribution are quantitatively determined and summarized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f for both Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes at various scan rates. Although the diffusion-dominated ratio decreases with increasing scan rates, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N has a higher fraction at all scan rates, indicating its excellent solid diffusion capability from the mesostructure with thin walls. The enhanced contact surface area with electrolyte, the interconnected mesopores, thin Mo<sub>2</sub>N walls, and the nanospace enclosed by mesopores plus its inherent high conductivity lead to the outstanding performance of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N in Na storage compared with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the improvement of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity within meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N <i>versus</i> bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) were evaluated according to the CV curves at different sweeping rates. The positive and negative shifts of the anodic and cathodic peaks in voltages with incremental sweep rates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5a</a>), respectively, mean an increasing polarization at higher scanning speed due to the kinetic limitations related to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the active material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The linearity of the peak current densities (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) with the square root of sweep speeds (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>), as described in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c,d</a>, indicates that Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion/extraction is a diffusion-dominated process. <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values are 2.16 × 10<sup>–13</sup> and 4.89 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for peaks A and C (Randles–Sevcik equation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a>), respectively, larger than those of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (e.g., peak A: 1.45 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, peak C: 4.14 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>).</div><div class="NLM_p">In light of the outstanding performances of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N in half-configuration, we coupled with home-made Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (NVP/C) as a cathode for full SIBs <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> to further explore its potential for practical application. The ratio of loading active materials within cathodes and anodes was 3:1 in weight in a typical full cell. The full-cell NVP/C∥meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N was cycled between 1.0 and 3.3 V at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. An average operating voltage of ≈2.15 V could be achieved from the representative charge/discharge profiles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The cycling capacity of full cell undergoes an observable decay in the initial 150 cycles and then levels off until 500 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). Although the porous nanochannels are still observed, the partial structure encircled with yellow circles could not recover its pristine structure after repeated insertion/extraction (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), which is probably one reason for capacity decay. After cycling 500 times, we still attained a stable cycling capacity of 83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an average CE of &gt;99%.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Galvanostatic charge and discharge profiles and (b) cycling performance of the full battery with meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N∥NVP/C couple at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The structural advantages can also be confirmed by Li storage in LIBs (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8–S11</a>). The possible Li storage mechanism in Mo<sub>2</sub>N is depicted as Mo<sub>2</sub>N + 3Li<sup>+</sup> ↔ 2Mo + Li<sub>3</sub>N, as revealed by ex situ XRD (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), in which the Li<sub>3</sub>N and Mo with less intense peaks can be detected at the discharge state. The capacity of 537/356 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained in the first discharge/charge cycle with an initial CE of 66.4% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8c</a>). As to the cyclic constancy in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8e</a>, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N cycles steadily at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> within 400 cycles while maintaining a high and steady capacity of 536 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. High reversible capacities of 505, 442, 378, 337, 309, and 230 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> are retained at stepwise current densities from 0.1 to 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. A capacity of 520 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is still maintained upon the current density returning to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating a superior reversibility of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N for Li<sup>+</sup> storage, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8f</a>. Upgrading to 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a stable and reversible capacity of 350 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> could be maintained for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N after cycling 2000 times (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8h</a>). Notably, the prominent cycling performances of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N outperform those of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and are much higher than those of metal nitrides for LIBs in previous reports (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>). This is superficially ascribed to the stable SEI film and the smaller charge-transfer resistance for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N in contrast with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b–f</a>). Most of mesostructure was retained after 10th (de)lithiation; however, the partial structure encircled with yellow circles was deformed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>).</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/medium/am9b07060_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. In LIBs, (a) CV curves of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Rate performance of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, the inset is the enlarged rate performances of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. (c) GITT profiles of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N with relaxation for 4 h after 1 h discharge/charge at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Internal resistances between charge and discharge steps after 4 h relaxation for these two electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/asset/images/large/am9b07060_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07060&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The GITT data reveal that meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N has smaller overpotentials and lower reaction resistances in comparison with bulk one (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c,d), suggesting the more favorable thermodynamics and kinetics in the (de)lithiation processes of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. In addition, meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N always keeps a higher diffusion-controlled contribution at all scan rates than that of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, indicating its rapid solid diffusion capability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a–f</a>). This is consistent with the situation of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N as an anode in SIBs. Also, Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Li</sub>) of peaks A and C are 3.18 × 10<sup>–13</sup> and 9.23 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, higher than those of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N (e.g., peak A: 4.4 × 10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, peak C: 3.71 × 10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). These results further verify that the high Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion derives from the thin-wall mesostructure of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A 3D mesoporous thin-wall Mo<sub>2</sub>N was constructed and the origins of its fast-charging were illustrated in detail. The thin-wall mesostructure of Mo<sub>2</sub>N in addition to its prominent inherent conductivity endows it with outstanding cyclability and excellent rate capability in contrast with bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N. The prominent electrochemical reaction kinetics of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N as promising electrode materials are attributed to rapid ions/electrons transportation, favorable ability of buffering volume variations and stable SEI films, endowing fast kinetics for (de)sodiation/(de)lithiation. The results highlight the enormous prospect of the thin-wall mesostructure, especially nitrides, in advanced electrochemical energy storage systems.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The experimental details of the synthesis process of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N, material characterizations, battery assembly, and electrochemical tests of SIBs and LIBs with meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrode are supplied in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b07060" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b07060</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Synthesis and characterization of materials, cell assembly, and electrochemical tests of SIBs and LIBs; pore size distribution of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N; TEM image of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N; CV curves of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N; galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N; galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at various rates for meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N in SIBs; SEM images of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N electrodes before and after 500 cycles in SIBs and LIBs; the partial electrochemical performance of meso-Mo<sub>2</sub>N and bulk-Mo<sub>2</sub>N in LIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07060/suppl_file/am9b07060_si_001.pdf">am9b07060_si_001.pdf (1.49 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b07060" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Chem. Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>51</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">290</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">298</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.7b00506</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D51%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D290-298%26journal%3DAcc.%2BChem.%2BRes.%26author%3DE.%2BHu%26author%3DX.%2BL.%2BWang%26author%3DX.%2BQ.%2BYu%26author%3DX.%2BQ.%2BYang%26title%3DProbing%2Bthe%2BComplexities%2Bof%2BStructural%2BChanges%2Bin%2BLayered%2BOxide%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BLi-ion%2BBatteries%2Bduring%2BFast%2BCharge-Discharge%2BCycling%2Band%2BHeating%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.accounts.7b00506&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b07060&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.accounts.7b00506&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Complexities of Structural Changes in Layered Oxide Cathode Materials for Li-Ion Batteries during Fast Charge-Discharge Cycling and Heating</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Enyuan; Wang, Xuelong; Yu, Xiqian; Yang, Xiao-Qing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">51</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">290-298</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rechargeable lithium-ion battery (LIB) is the most promising energy storage system to power elec. vehicles with high energy d. and long cycling life.  However, in order to meet customers' demands for fast charging, the power performances of current LIBs need to be improved.  From the cathode aspect, layer-structured cathode materials are widely used in today's market and will continue to play important roles in the near future.  The high rate capability of layered cathode materials during charging and discharging is crit. to the power performance of the whole cell and the thermal stability is closely related to the safety issues.  Therefore, the in-depth understanding of structural changes of layered cathode materials during high rate charging/discharging and the thermal stability during heating are essential in developing new materials and improving current materials.  Since structural changes take place from the at. level to the whole electrode level, combination of characterization techniques covering multilength scales is quite important.  In many cases, this means using comprehensive tools involving diffraction, spectroscopy, and imaging to differentiate the surface from the bulk and to obtain structural/chem. information with different levels of spatial resoln.For example, hard X-ray spectroscopy can yield the bulk information and soft X-ray spectroscopy can give the surface information; X-ray based imaging techniques can obtain spatial resoln. of tens of nanometers, and electron-based microcopy can go to angstroms.  In addn. to challenges assocd. with different spatial resoln., the dynamic nature of structural changes during high rate cycling and heating requires characterization tools to have the capability of collecting high quality data in a time-resolved fashion.  Thanks to the advancement in synchrotron based techniques and high-resoln. electron microscopy, high temporal and spatial resolns. can now be achieved.  In this Account, we focus on the recent works studying kinetic and thermal properties of layer-structured cathode materials, esp. the structural changes during high rate cycling and the thermal stability during heating.  Advanced characterization techniques relating to the rate capability and thermal stability will be introduced.  The different structure evolution behavior of cathode materials cycled at high rate will be compared with that cycled at low rate.  Different response of individual transition metals and the inhomogeneity in chem. distribution will be discussed.  For the thermal stability, the relationship between structural changes and oxygen release will be emphatically pointed out.  In all these studies being reviewed, advanced characterization techniques are critically applied to reveal complexities at multiscale in layer-structured cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqvcjf1DH_-eLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljs87nZmIsm3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtFOnsr4%253D&amp;md5=a36ac473560b142eb24679f0f6080434</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-017-00973-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chae, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ko, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fast-charging High-energy Lithium-ion Batteries via Implantation of Amorphous Silicon Nanolayer in Edge-plane Activated Graphite Anodes</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fast-charging high-energy lithium-ion batteries via implantation of amorphous silicon nanolayer in edge-plane activated graphite anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim Namhyung; Chae Sujong; Ma Jiyoung; Ko Minseong; Cho Jaephil; Ko Minseong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">812</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">As fast-charging lithium-ion batteries turn into increasingly important components in forthcoming applications, various strategies have been devoted to the development of high-rate anodes.  However, despite vigorous efforts, the low initial Coulombic efficiency and poor volumetric energy density with insufficient electrode conditions remain critical challenges that have to be addressed.  Herein, we demonstrate a hybrid anode via incorporation of a uniformly implanted amorphous silicon nanolayer and edge-site-activated graphite.  This architecture succeeds in improving lithium ion transport and minimizing initial capacity losses even with increase in energy density.  As a result, the hybrid anode exhibits an exceptional initial Coulombic efficiency (93.8%) and predominant fast-charging behavior with industrial electrode conditions.  As a result, a full-cell demonstrates a higher energy density (≥1060 Wh l(-1)) without any trace of lithium plating at a harsh charging current density (10.2 mA cm(-2)) and 1.5 times faster charging than that of conventional graphite.It is desirable to develop fast-charging batteries retaining high energy density.  Here, the authors report a hybrid anode via incorporation of an implanted amorphous silicon nanolayer and edge-plane-activated graphite, which meets both criteria.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRogyN6q6G8-TW1zwg2yUK5fW6udTcc2eZuTTqXAiF6tLntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1M%252FmvVKrtQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=d0fbda29b078ca79e7ed96b3ae5428d0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/srep08225"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemically Active, Crystalline, Mesoporous Covalent Organic Frameworks on Carbon Nanotubes for Synergistic Lithium-ion Battery Energy Storage</span>. <i>Sci. Rep.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">8225</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemically active, crystalline, mesoporous covalent organic frameworks on carbon nanotubes for synergistic lithium-ion battery energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Fei; Jin, Shangbin; Zhong, Hui; Wu, Dingcai; Yang, Xiaoqing; Chen, Xiong; Wei, Hao; Fu, Ruowen; Jiang, Donglin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific Reports</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8225</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SRCEC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2045-2322</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Org. batteries free of toxic metal species could lead to a new generation of consumer energy storage devices that are safe and environmentally benign.  However, the conventional org. electrodes remain problematic because of their structural instability, slow ion-diffusion dynamics, and poor elec. cond.  Here, we report on the development of a redox-active, cryst., mesoporous covalent org. framework (COF) on carbon nanotubes for use as electrodes; the electrode stability is enhanced by the covalent network, the ion transport is facilitated by the open meso-channels, and the electron cond. is boosted by the carbon nanotube wires.  These effects work synergistically for the storage of energy and provide lithium-ion batteries with high efficiency, robust cycle stability, and high rate capability.  Our results suggest that redox-active COFs on conducting carbons could serve as a unique platform for energy storage and may facilitate the design of new org. electrodes for high-performance and environmentally benign battery devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqZxFLwMDgUmLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljs87nZmIsm3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtFKjurnK&amp;md5=00c74a4fecbdd21ea4a63b50ac77d3a2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b00336"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extended π-Conjugated System for Fast-Charge and -Discharge Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Chengliang; Xu, Yang; Fang, Yaoguo; Zhou, Min; Liang, Liying; Singh, Sukhdeep; Zhao, Huaping; Schober, Andreas; Lei, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Org. sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are potential alternatives of current com. inorg. lithium-ion batteries for portable electronics (esp. wearable electronics) because of their low cost and flexibility, making them possible to meet the future flexible and large-scale requirements.  However, only a few org. SIBs have been reported so far, and most of them either were tested in a very slow rate or suffered significant performance degrdn. when cycled under high rate.  Here, we are focusing on the mol. design for improving the battery performance and addressing the current challenge of fast-charge and -discharge.  Through reasonable mol. design strategy, we demonstrate that the extension of the π-conjugated system is an efficient way to improve the high rate performance, leading to much enhanced capacity and cyclability with full recovery even after cycled under c.d. as high as 10 A g-1.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrEuEFa5C1W2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljs87nZmIsm3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitlWmurk%253D&amp;md5=5fe3b24da903a9753cd0ae510f20eb2e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201802423"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, M.
